    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="c5a387715e5d4e2d9cca2dfafbd180b6">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'df8aaee5-3298-4754-8072-ffcbd1a8bb8a'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d43537248" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Quasi-layered sodium titanates have been extensively studied as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) owing to their quasi-zero-strain intercalative storage chemistry and high theoretical capacity. However, their sluggish sodiation kinetics and unstable electrode/electrolyte interface lead to rapid capacity decay at low temperatures. Herein, the local electronic structure and interlayer spacing of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> are finely regulated by heteroelement Sn-doping, oxygen rich vacancies, and carbon-confined structure (Sn-HNTO@C) to improve low-temperature performance. Theoretical calculations and Sn doping concentration control confirm that appropriate concentrations of heteroelement Sn-doping and vacancy defects can redistribute charge density, enhance Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption, reduce Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barriers, and endow Sn-HNTO@C anode with stable capacity. In addition, optimizing electrolyte systems at low temperatures allows Sn-HNTO@C to exhibit a Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent co-intercalation storage mechanism in ether-based electrolytes, avoiding high desolvent energy barriers and reducing charge transfer activation energy. Furthermore, the thin, stable solid electrolyte interface rich in organic components promotes the low-temperature interfacial Na<sup>+</sup> kinetics. Consequently, Sn-HNTO@C anode delivers high capacity over 500 cycles (177 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and Sn-HNTO@C//Na<sub>3</sub>(VPO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> full cell presents 91 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> over 200 cycles (−15&nbsp;°C). This study provides unique guidance for optimizing sodium titanate anodes and emphasizes the importance of the low-temperature electrode/electrolyte interface for SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202417725-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202417725-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The growing demand for grid storage, space exploration, and electric vehicles has driven researchers to focus on developing high-performance energy storage systems at low temperatures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0001_R_d43537240e422" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Mater.">1</a>, <a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0002_R_d43537240e422" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been widely employed in various electronic products, their rapid capacity decline and cycle life degradation at high current densities in cold climates still hinder their applications.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0003_R_d43537240e427" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0004_R_d43537240e427" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Commun.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> SIBs have significant advantages in reducing costs, improving safety, and mitigating environmental impacts, making them an important supplement or even substitute for LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0005_R_d43537240e432" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - ACS Nano">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is worth noting that the low desolvation energy of Na<sup>+</sup> (≈25–30% lower than Li<sup>+</sup>) results in a lower activation barrier during Na<sup>+</sup> (de)insertion, suggesting faster ion diffusion capability and superior sodium storage performance at low temperatures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0006_R_d43537240e444" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Chem. Rev.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, it is imperative to develop low-temperature SIBs with satisfactory specific capacity, long cycle life, and high safety.</p>
         
         <p>At present, the main reasons hindering the low-temperature sodium storage performance are as follows: i) When the temperature is below 0&nbsp;°C, the electrolyte tends to solidify, resulting in a decrease in the wettability and ionic conductivity, which hinders ion transport speed in the electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0007_R_d43537240e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> ii) During the charging and discharging process, it is difficult to desolvate Na<sup>+</sup> at the electrode/electrolyte interface, resulting in an increase in interface charge transfer resistance, which slows down the transmission of Na<sup>+</sup> through the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film and inside the electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0008_R_d43537240e461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nat. Energy">8</a>, <a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0009_R_d43537240e461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - ACS Nano">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is noteworthy that the interface Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation step is more sensitive at low temperatures, and its high-energy barrier is the main step determining the transfer rate at low temperatures, inducing high interface charge transfer impedance, resulting in slow kinetic transfer inside the SEI film and electrode for SIBs, thereby greatly reducing the energy density and cycling performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0010_R_d43537240e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Storage Mater.">10</a>, <a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0011_R_d43537240e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - ACS Energy Lett.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To solve the above problems, researchers have made great efforts from the perspective of electrolyte engineering,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0012_R_d43537240e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Energy Environ. Sci.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> such as using weakly polar solvents or adjusting solvation structures to promote the desolvation process, thereby improving the rate performance and low-temperature performance of embedded carbon-based anode electrodes to a certain extent.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0013_R_d43537240e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ACS Nano">13</a>, <a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0014_R_d43537240e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - ACS Energy Lett.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, severe polarization at high current density can lead to rapid reaching of the cut-off voltage, resulting in low capacity and severe sodium dendrite formation at low temperatures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0015_R_d43537240e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">15</a>, <a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0016_R_d43537240e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Environ. Sci.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, developing advanced materials with fast interface dynamics and suitable electrolyte systems is the main challenge for low-temperature SIBs, which is of great significance for promoting the development of low-temperature SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Among numerous anode materials, quasi-layered Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>x</sub>O<sub>2x+1</sub> (<i>x</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;2,3,4,6,7) have attracted increasing extensive attention for SIBs due to their suitable atomic spacing, low lattice strain, high theoretical capacity, and good electrochemical stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0017_R_d43537240e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Nano">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (NTO) with quasi-zero-strain intercalative storage chemistry, low operating voltage (≈0.3 V), and high energy density is considered a new ideal anode material. Compared with graphite anodes, NTO exhibits a stable structural framework and larger interlayer spacing, which is expected to maintain structural stability during Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent insertion/extraction and achieve cycling stability at low temperatures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0018_R_d43537240e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Mater.">18</a>, <a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0019_R_d43537240e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Nano">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the wide band gap of the NTO results in poor ion/electron conductivity, and the slow transport kinetics cause inferior low-temperature rate performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0018_R_d43537240e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Mater.">18</a>, <a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0020_R_d43537240e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Energy Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the cycling process, the partial decomposition of the NTO surface also destabilizes the SEI film and the repeated insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent will cause the aggregation and stacking of NTO, and these unfavorable factors seriously restrict the low temperature practical application of NTO.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0021_R_d43537240e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Energy Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In response to the above issues, the current modification strategies mainly focus on surface control such as nanostructure design and composite with highly conductive carbon materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0022_R_d43537240e534" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Nano">22</a>, <a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0023_R_d43537240e534" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Rare Met.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the internal electronic structure in these strategies has not been optimized, which cannot improve the intrinsic conductivity of NTO, causing phase interface side reactions, hindering the rapid transfer of ions/electrons inside the electrode material, resulting in poor long-term cycling and high current charge/discharge performance. Therefore, to achieve rapid ion/electron transport, fine control of the electronic structure and interlayer spacing of NTO is crucial for enhancing the low-temperature interface dynamics. In general, defect engineering can greatly improve the physical and chemical properties of electrode materials,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0024_R_d43537240e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> where oxygen vacancies (OV) can enhance ion/electron conductivity and provide abundant active sites for Na<sup>+</sup>, increasing pseudocapacitive behavior.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0025_R_d43537240e547" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Coord. Chem. Rev.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Heteroelement doping strategy is another effective method, which can not only fundamentally regulate the transport channels and electron distribution, but also suppress lattice structure transformation and reduce internal stress.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0026_R_d43537240e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Huo et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0027_R_d43537240e557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Sep. Purif. Technol.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> reported porous carbon with controllable N doping and carbon defects combined to achieve excellent capacitive deionization performance, demonstrating that doping and defect interactions are beneficial for improving ion adsorption and diffusion. Wang et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0028_R_d43537240e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> also proposed a strategy of nitrogen doping combined with Te vacancies for fast and stable potassium ion storage. The current research results show that combining heterogeneous metal element doping and vacancy defect strategies can expand the interlayer spacing, reduce ion diffusion barriers, ensure structural stability, and accelerate interface reaction kinetics at room temperature. However, the influence mechanism of heteroelement doping and OV on the low-temperature interface dynamics and SEI composition and structure is still unclear. It is unknown whether there is a Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent co-intercalation storage mechanism at low temperatures and the specific structural evolution during Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent insertion/extraction processes.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we report the design and preparation of heteroelement Sn-doping and oxygen vacancy rich Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> ultrathin nanosheets confined in porous hollow carbon spheres (Sn-HNTO@C) to improve low-temperature interface dynamics and sodium storage performance. Theoretical calculations show that heteroelement Sn-doping and abundant OV reduce the band gap of NTO to ensure effective ion transport, improve intrinsic conductivity, and provide more active sites for sodium storage, thereby achieving rapid charge transfer. As expected, Sn-HNTO@C anode demonstrates outstanding rate performance, with a capacity of 108 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> at room temperature, and maintains a capacity of 72 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 3 A g<sup>−1</sup> even under −15&nbsp;°C. When coupling with Na<sub>3</sub>(VPO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> cathode, the Sn-HNTO@C//Na<sub>3</sub>(VPO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> full cell also exhibits satisfying rate capability and outstanding low-temperature electrochemical performance. By optimizing the electrolyte solvent structure and testing the SEI layer composition, it was found that the diglyme-based electrolytes contribute to forming thin and stable organic–inorganic SEI layers, effectively overcoming the slow interfacial dynamics and significantly improving the low-temperature electrochemical performance. In addition, compared to traditional ester electrolytes, Sn-HNTO@C anode in diglyme-based electrolytes exhibits a Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent co-intercalation mechanism and low desolvent energy barriers, resulting in faster reaction kinetics at low temperatures.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202417725-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202417725-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202417725-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202417725-sec-0030-title">2.1 Theoretical Calculation</h3>
            
            <p>To gain a more comprehensive understanding of the relationship between oxygen defects, heteroelement doping, and Na<sup>+</sup> storage, density functional theory (DFT) calculations were first used to evaluate the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism of Sn-HNTO@C from an atomic point of view. Four molecular structures of NTO, NTO with OV (NTOV), Sn-doping NTO (NTOS), and Sn-doping NTO with OV (NTOVS) were constructed (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0001">1a−d</a> illustrates the differential charge density (DCD) for the four model structures. Electronic structure visualization images show a more densely distributed charge region in the NTOVS system compared to NTO, NTOV, and NTOS, where the charge accumulation region near OV is larger and Na<sup>+</sup> will lose more electrons. Bader charge analysis exactly quantified the number of lost electrons, with a maximum of 0.64 electrons lost in NTOVS, suggesting that Sn-doping and the OV provide more reversible storage sites for Na<sup>+</sup> and enhance the adsorption capacity of Na<sup>+</sup>. As depicted in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0001">1e–h</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a–d</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202417725-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c93e3dd1-3673-4a5c-9fff-82206de8378d/adfm202417725-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c93e3dd1-3673-4a5c-9fff-82206de8378d/adfm202417725-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/762ca79e-1fc1-437d-9866-cd20cbbfa167/adfm202417725-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c93e3dd1-3673-4a5c-9fff-82206de8378d/adfm202417725-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202417725-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202417725" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Theoretical calculation and analyses. a−d) Charge density difference of NTO, NTOV, NTOS, and NTOVS. e–h) Calculated DOS analyses of NTO, NTOV, NTOS, and NTOVS. i) Adsorption energy (<i>ΔE<sub>ads</sub></i>) for Na storage in all calculated models. j) The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion path for NTO, NTOV, NTOS, and NTOVS. k) Corresponding diffusion energy barrier.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Compared to NTO (4.02 eV), NTOV (3.90 eV), and NTOS (3.67 eV), the simultaneous introduction of Sn doping and OV gives rise to a significant defective state in the NTOVS (3.63 eV), resulting in its band gap minimum. This result suggests that Sn-doping and OV enhance the DOS near the Fermi energy level, increasing the electrical conductivity and accelerating the transport of ions and electrons. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0001">1i</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information) show the <i>ΔE<sub>ads</sub></i> values of the four structures, and the NTOVS model exhibits the largest negative adsorption energy value, suggesting that OV and Sn-doping promote Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption, which is in agreement with the analysis of the DCD and Bader charge. In addition, the diffusion behavior of Na<sup>+</sup> in four structural models is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0001">1j</a>. It can be found that NTOVS exhibits the lowest diffusion energy barrier (0.45 eV), remarkably lower than NTO (0.55 eV), NTOV (0.52 eV), and NTOS (0.48 eV), suggesting the fast diffusion rate of Na<sup>+</sup> in NTOVS (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0001">1k</a>). Based on the DFT calculations, compared with the single OV and Sn doping strategy, the synergistic strategy of OV and Sn-doping in the NTO system significantly enhances the conductivity, achieves fast charge transfer, and lowers the diffusion energy barrier of Na<sup>+</sup>, which will lead to the excellent electrochemical performance of NTO.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202417725-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202417725-sec-0040-title">2.2 Synthesis and Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>Combined with the results of theoretical calculations, we prepared Sn-HNTO@C to further investigate its Na<sup>+</sup> storage properties. The synthetic process of Sn-HNTO@C composite is depicted in <b>Figures</b> <a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0002">2a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). First, SiO<sub>2</sub> spheres were prepared with a diameter of ≈250 nm by a classic Stöber method (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). Second, the TiO<sub>2</sub> and RF layers were sequentially coated on the surface of SiO<sub>2</sub> using the stirring method at room temperature and converted to carbon layers by calcination (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5–S7</a>, Supporting Information). Finally, the Sn-HNTO@C composite was synthesized by hydrothermal method and subsequent calcination in an argon-hydrogen atmosphere. In this unique architecture of Sn-HNTO@C, the ultra-thin NTO nanosheets with abundant OV narrow the band gap, improve the conductivity, and provide sufficient active sites for redox reactions. The heteroelement Sn-doping further expands the interlayer spacing of NTO and realizes the rapid ion migration. In addition, the hollow carbon sphere structure effectively mitigates the severe volume changes during the sodiation and desodiation process and enhances the structural stability of Sn-HNTO active material. These synergistic effects endow Sn-HNTO@C electrode with better stability and superior rate performance during cycling. Besides, NTO, HNTO, HNTO@C, and Sn-NTO@C were prepared as comparisons to evaluate the characteristic advantages of Sn-HNTO@C.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202417725-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a3a1e020-5830-4e8d-a5c5-d1cd4a21e88a/adfm202417725-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a3a1e020-5830-4e8d-a5c5-d1cd4a21e88a/adfm202417725-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/146d45fb-fe73-46bf-88fe-af5129a77cd7/adfm202417725-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a3a1e020-5830-4e8d-a5c5-d1cd4a21e88a/adfm202417725-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202417725-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202417725" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration of synthetic route for the Sn-HNTO@C nanospheres. b) SEM images, c) TEM images, d,e) HRTEM images, f) TEM-EDS element mapping images, g–j) TOF-SIMS 3D images for Sn, Na, Ti, and O elements of Sn-HNTO@C. k) XRD patterns of all samples. l) XRD patterns of the enlarged (200) peak for HNTO@C and Sn-HNTO@C. m) Raman spectrum of HNTO, HNTO@C and Sn-HNTO@C. XPS spectra: n) Na 1s of HNTO@C and Sn-HNTO@C, o) Sn 3d of Sn-HNTO@C. p) Ti 2p of HNTO@C and Sn-HNTO@C. q) EPR spectra of HNTO, HNTO@C, Sn-NTO@C, and Sn-HNTO@C. r) plots of (<i>αhν</i>)<i><sup>1/2</sup></i> versus photon energy (<i>hv</i>) of all samples.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images show that Sn-HNTO@C, Sn-NTO@C, and HNTO@C nanospheres exhibit more complete spherical shell structures due to the porous carbon spheres formed by RF layer carbonation compared to HNTO and NTO samples (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0002">2b</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a,b</a>, Supporting Information). The transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images indicate that the ultra-thin Sn-HNTO nanosheets are uniformly filled in porous hollow carbon spheres (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0002">2c</a>). Meanwhile, there is no significant difference in morphology between Sn-HNTO@C and HNTO@C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9c,d</a>, Supporting Information), which proves that the Sn-doping at this level still maintains a complete spherical structure. The high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0002">2d,e</a> vividly shows a lattice distance of 0.74 nm, which correlates well to the (200) plane of the NTO. Compared with HNTO@C, the lattice spacing of Sn-HNTO@C is significantly enlarged (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9e,f</a>, Supporting Information), further revealing that the Sn substitution has successfully expanded the backbone layer spacing of NTO. Energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) elemental mappings verified that C and N elements were evenly distributed on the surface of the nanosphere, while Sn, Na, Ti, and O elements were concentrated in the interior of the nanosphere (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0002">2f</a>). HNTO@C shows the distribution of Na, Ti, O, C, and N elements (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9g</a>, Supporting Information). Time of flight secondary ion mass spectrometry (TOF-SIMS) was used to further analyze the elemental component and distribution of Sn-HNTO@C. The change of main elements in the sample with sputtering time further confirms the successful doping of Sn elements (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). The uniform distribution of the Sn, Na, Ti, and O four elements (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0002">2g–j</a>) corresponds to the EDS element mappings.</p>
            
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0002">2k</a> shows the crystal structure characteristics of the NTO, HNTO, HNTO@C, Sn-NTO@C, and Sn-HNTO@C samples using X-ray diffraction (XRD). The main characteristic peaks match well with the standard card data (JCPDS No. 47–0124), indicating the same crystal structure. Notably, the (200) characteristic peak of HNTO is shifted to the right relative to NTO, which is mainly due to its dehydration during high-temperature annealing, resulting in a decrease in the spacing of the crystalline surfaces. In addition, the (200) peak of Sn-HNTO@C was shifted from 9.56° to 9.38° (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0002">2l</a>), compared to that of HNTO@C, indicating that the introduction of Sn doping leads to an increase in lattice spacing. The peaks appearing at positions 280, 438, 708, and 902 cm<sup>−1</sup> in the Raman spectra are assigned to the inherent peaks of NTO (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0002">2m</a>). The characteristic peaks at 280 and 438 cm<sup>−1</sup> belong to the A<sub>1g</sub> bending vibration of Na–O–Ti in NTO, and the peaks approximately at 708 and 902 cm<sup>−1</sup> are attributed to the vibration of O–Ti–O.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0021_R_d43537240e821" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Energy Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The disappearance of the peak at 280 cm<sup>−1</sup> for Sn-HNTO@C may be associated with the disruption of the Na─O─Ti bond as a result of Sn-doping replacing part of the Ti sites. The carbon content of Sn-HNTO@C was ≈44.1% by thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). The chemical composition of Sn-HNTO@C was further studied by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). The high-resolution Na 1s spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0002">2n</a>) confirm the presence of NTO crystal, and the appearance of the Sn 3d spectrum is another strong evidence for the successful doping of Sn elements (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0002">2o</a>). The deconvolution of the C1s spectra shows four peaks situating at 282.6, 284.8, 285.9, and 288.7 eV correspond to the chemical states of C–Ti, C–C, C–N, and C–O, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12a</a>, Supporting Information). Among them, the C─Ti bonds in Sn-HNTO@C play a particularly important role in stabilizing OV and ensuring the integrity of electrode structures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0029_R_d43537240e841" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The binding energies at 398.5 and 400.2 eV for the N 1s spectrum should assign to pyridinic N and pyrrolic N, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12b</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0030_R_d43537240e850" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - ACS Energy Lett.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The presence of C─N bonds and nitrogen-doped sites can facilitate the electrical conductivity of the carbon layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0031_R_d43537240e855" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nano-Micro Lett.">31</a>, <a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0032_R_d43537240e855" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - InfoMat">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peaks of Ti<sup>4+</sup> species with Ti 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 2p<sub>1/2</sub> signals at 457.6 and 463.5 eV for HNTO@C are shifted to 457.7 and 463.6 eV for Sn-HNTO@C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0002">2p</a>), and more Ti<sup>3+</sup> species have been detected in Sn-HNTO@C sample. In the O 1s spectrum, the peaks at 531.7 eV produced from OV (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12c</a>, Supporting Information),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0033_R_d43537240e875" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Energy Storage Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and more OV are present in Sn-HNTO@C compared to HNTO@C. These results suggest that Sn-doping contributes to the generation of Ti<sup>3+</sup> and OV. Furthermore, additional components are deconvoluted at 534.4 eV in the O 1s spectra, being ascribed to the presence of surface functional groups (–OH).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0034_R_d43537240e883" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The electronic conductivity of the samples was evaluated through the implementation of the four-probe method. Compared to HNTO@C and Sn-NTO@C, the Sn-HNTO@C shows the highest conductivity, indicating that Sn-doping and OV play a crucial role in increasing the conductivity (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). Electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) was conducted (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0002">2q</a>) to ascertain the presence of OV. Compared with Sn-NTO@C, HNTO@C, and HNTO, a significant signal was observed at g&nbsp;=&nbsp;2.005 for Sn-HNTO@C, indicating that the synergistic effect of Sn-doping and OV assisted the formation of oxygen vacancy rich NTO. In addition, the band structure changes induced by OV were verified by ultraviolet-visible diffuse reflectance spectra (UV–vis/DRs). A significant increase in absorbance was observed for Sn-HNTO@C, especially in the visible light region (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). The band gap of the five samples was obtained by analyzing the plots between the absorption coefficient (<i>α</i>) and the photon energy (<i>hv</i>). The band gap values of the NTO, HNTO, HNTO@C, and Sn-NTO@C are 3.60, 3.46, 2.28, and 1.84 eV, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0002">2r</a>). After the introduction of Sn-doping and OV, the band gap value is further reduced to 1.77 eV, which is significantly lower than that of the remaining four samples. Such a low band gap is more conducive to enhancing the electronic conductivity and ensuring faster charge transfer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0035_R_d43537240e907" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Energy Mater.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The nitrogen adsorption/desorption isotherms and pore size distribution indicate the high specific surface area and mesoporous structure of Sn-HNTO@C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information), which facilitates electrolyte penetration (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information), and promotes rapid electron/ion transfer.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202417725-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202417725-sec-0050-title">2.3 Room Temperature Sodium Storage Performance and Mechanism</h3>
            
            <p>The sodium storage performance of the Sn-HNTO@C anode was investigated through the half-cell assembly. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0003">3a</a> shows the initial three cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of Sn-HNTO@C at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. During the cathodic sweep, the initial reduction peak at 0.98 V vanished in subsequent scans, which is associated with the electrolyte decomposition and the formation of SEI film. The CV curves in subsequent scans show typical NTO anode behavior with broad redox peaks, which correspond to the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/extraction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0036_R_d43537240e937" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - SusMat">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The fine overlap of CV curves also suggests a stable reversibility of the Sn-HNTO@C anode. The initial five cycles of galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) curves for Sn-HNTO@C are illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0003">3b</a>, providing a charge/discharge capacity of 629/298 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in the first cycle, corresponding to an initial coulomb efficiency (ICE) of 47.4%. The low ICE is mainly due to the SEI formation related to the oxidation-reduction reaction between the electrode and the electrolyte in large-area contact, increased reactivity of the OV, and irreversible Na-ion intercalation at the OV and heteroatom positions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0037_R_d43537240e948" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Energy Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The low ICE may result in the extensive utilization of cathodes and electrolytes for SIB full cells, which could subsequently impact the energy density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0038_R_d43537240e954" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Electrochem. Energy Rev.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The low ICE can be attenuated through the optimization of electrolytes, anode pre-sodiation, sodium-rich cathodes, and so on.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0039_R_d43537240e959" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Energy Storage Mater.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The CV curves of Sn-NTO@C, HNTO@C, HNTO, and NTO are illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17a–d</a> (Supporting Information), and the graph's shape is largely consistent with that of Sn-HNTO@C, likely due to the analogous reaction, corresponding to that is their GCD curves (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17e–h</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202417725-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a923e471-6bd1-460a-a681-092faf84133c/adfm202417725-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a923e471-6bd1-460a-a681-092faf84133c/adfm202417725-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3aa7fd75-2b76-4649-aee4-f05677207983/adfm202417725-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a923e471-6bd1-460a-a681-092faf84133c/adfm202417725-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202417725-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202417725" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical characterizations in SIB half and full cells. a) CV curves of Sn-HNTO@C. b) GCD curves for the first five cycles of Sn-HNTO@C electrode. c) Cycling performance at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and d) rate performance of Sn-HNTO@C and contrast electrodes. e) Cycling performance at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> of Sn-HNTO@C and contrast electrodes. f) Cycling performance of Sn-HNTO@C at 3 A g<sup>−1</sup>. g) Comparison of electrochemical properties between Sn-HNTO@C electrode and previously published NTO-based anode materials. h) Ex situ XRD patterns of Sn-HNTO@C electrode for the first two cycles. i) Ex situ XPS spectra of Sn-HNTO@C for the initial cycle. j) Configurations of Sn-HNTO@C SIB full cell. k) GCD curves of SIB half and full cells. l) Cycling stability and m) rate performance of the Sn-HNTO@C//NVPF full cell (inset: photograph of lighted LED bulbs). n) Ragone plots in comparison with other reported full cells.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To explore the relationship between structure and sodium storage performance, five samples were employed. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0003">3c</a> shows the cycle performance of different electrodes at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. After 500 cycles, the specific capacity of Sn-HNTO@C remains at 209 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, dramatically higher than other electrodes. The rate performance was tested and demonstrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0003">3d</a>. Obviously, Sn-HNTO@C reveals the highest capacity of 268 at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>, whereafter it exhibits specific capacities of 236, 202, 174, 153, 135, 124, and 108 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 3, and 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. In addition, when the current density was returned to 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the capacity was increased to 238 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, and the capacity retention was 89% compared to the initial state. While Sn-NTO@C, HNTO@C, HNTO, and NTO exhibit capacities 68, 54, 32, and 30 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, which is noticeably lower than Sn-HNTO@C. The GCD curves show that the polarisation of the Sn-HNTO@C electrode is smaller than that of the Sn-NTO@C, HNTO@C, HNTO, and NTO electrodes, confirming the superior ion/electron diffusion kinetics of Sn-HNTO@C electrode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, at a high current density of 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, Sn-HNTO@C displays the best cycling performance, with 124 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles, high than of NTO (39 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), HNTO (58mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), HNTO@C (84 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), and Sn-NTO@C (100 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) electrodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0003">3e</a>). Surprisingly, when increasing the current density to 3 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the Sn-HNTO@C electrode maintains a capacity of 52 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 8000 cycles, and CE was ≈ 100% after the first several cycles, indicating long-term cycling durability (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0003">3f</a>). In addition, the Sn-HNTO@C anode exhibits favorable cycle and rate performance compared with a series of NTO-based anodes for half-cells in the previous literature at room temperature (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0003">3g</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>Although the above results indicate that the synergistic effect of Sn doping and OV can enhance the sodium storage performance of the NTO anode, the relationship between the Sn doping amount and the oxygen vacancy content still needs to be studied. Therefore, Sn-HNTO@C with excess Sn doping amounts (E-Sn-HNTO@C) and skimpy Sn doping amounts (S-Sn-HNTO@C) were obtained. The actual content of Sn element in the three electrodes as determined by inductively coupled plasma mass spectrometry (ICP-MS) is shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). The SEM images show that the three materials still have a spherical structure, indicating that this doping level has little effect on the overall structure (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19a–c</a>, Supporting Information). XRD results indicate that as the Sn doping amount increases, the (200) peak shifts slightly toward lower angles, indicating an expansion of interlayer spacing (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19d</a>, Supporting Information). The EPR results indicate that as the Sn doping concentration increases, the peak becomes stronger, indicating a higher concentration of vacancy defects in the material (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19e</a>, Supporting Information). However, excessive concentration of defect vacancies can capture Na<sup>+</sup> in the electrolyte, causing irreversible Na<sup>+</sup> loss and resulting in a loss of electrode material capacity. Therefore, constructing a suitable vacancy concentration controlled by Sn doping in the crystal structure, and expanding the interlayer spacing, is expected to significantly improve ion transport. To further confirm the effect of Sn doping on sodium storage performance, these three materials as anodes were assembled into Na-ion batteries for comparison. The Sn-HNTO@C electrode outperforms cycle performance and rate capability compared with S-Sn-HNTO@C and E-Sn-HNTO@C electrodes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19f,g</a>, Supporting Information). EIS analysis manifests that Sn-HNTO@C possesses lower charge-transfer resistance (<i>R<sub>ct</sub></i>) as compared to those of S-Sn-HNTO@C and E-Sn-HNTO@C, demonstrating the enhanced reaction kinetics of Sn-HNTO@C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19h, i</a>, Supporting Information). This means that the appropriate concentration of Sn doping and OV can greatly enhance the electrochemical kinetics and greatly improve the rate performance. To elucidate the reaction mechanism at room temperature, ex situ XRD test was employed to document the structural evolution of the Sn-HNTO@C anode during the first cycle (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0003">3h</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information). The (200) diffraction peak shows a slight and reversible angular shift, and no new peak appears, indicating reversible deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup>. The valence changes of Ti in Sn-HNTO@C during charging and discharging were characterized by ex situ XPS (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0003">3i</a>). During discharge to 0.01 V, more Ti<sup>4+</sup> was reduced and the content of Ti<sup>3+</sup> increased significantly, while Ti<sup>3+</sup> was reversibly oxidized to Ti<sup>4+</sup> during the charging stage. This suggests that the Sn-HNTO@C electrode involves only Na<sup>+</sup> migration and conversion between Ti<sup>3+</sup> and Ti<sup>4+</sup> during cycling.</p>
            
            <p>The sodium-ion full cell with Sn-HNTO@C as the anode and Na<sub>3</sub>(VPO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> (NVPF) as the cathode was assembled to verify the potential applications of the Sn-HNTO@C electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0003">3j</a>). The morphology and composition of NVPF were revealed by SEM and XRD (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information), and its electrochemical properties are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a> (Supporting Information). To achieve the optimal performance of the full cell, the voltage window of 1–4 V was determined based on the discharge and charge platforms of Sn-HNTO@C and NVPF (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0003">3k</a>). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0003">3l</a> illustrates the cycling performance, retaining the capacity of 129 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles. Strikingly, the full cell after cycling can still allow the light-emitting diodes (LEDs) to glow (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0003">3l</a>). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0003">3m</a> exhibits the full cell rate performance with the average specific capacities of 176, 126, 105, 90, 74, 60, and 50 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.3, 0.5, 1, 2, and 3 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. When the current density returns to 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the capacity can be retrieved to 102 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. The GCD curves at different currents are displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a> (Supporting Information). In addition, the energy and power densities of the full cell were calculated, and as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0003">3n</a>, the Sn-HNTO@C//NVPF full cell demonstrates the maximum energy density of 325 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, which is better than previous work reported in the literature. The CV curves of the full cell at different sweep speeds maintain essentially the same shape, proving its good stability (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24a</a>, Supporting Information). The high capacitance contribution of up to 75% indicates a fast reaction kinetics, supporting its favorable rate performance (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24b,c</a>, Supporting Information). Overall, the excellent full-cell performance proves its potential for practical application.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202417725-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202417725-sec-0060-title">2.4 Room Temperature Sodium Storage Interfacial Kinetics</h3>
            
            <p>To study the sodium storage behavior of the Sn-HNTO@C electrode in more depth, the CV curves were tested at 0.1–5 mV s<sup>−1</sup> to evaluate the charge storage behavior and dynamics characteristics. CV curves with well-maintained shapes were obtained with increasing sweep speed, indicating a smaller polarization (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0004">4a</a>). According to the relationship between the peak current (<i>i</i>) and the sweep rate (<i>v</i>) (Equation <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information), the cathodic and anodic <i>b</i>-values for Sn-HNTO@C were calculated to be 0.87 and 0.89 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0004">4b</a>), respectively, higher than that of Sn-NTO@C, HNTO@C, HNTO, and NTO (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a>, Supporting Information). This finding confirms that Sn-doping and OV help improve the capacitive contribution behavior and ensure faster reaction kinetics. To quantitatively distinguish between relative capacitance and diffusion-controlled processes at specific voltages, the capacitance contribution was calculated (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a>, Supporting Information). Comparing the capacitive contribution of the five samples at varying sweep speeds reveals that Sn-HNTO@C exhibits the highest capacitive contribution at each scanning rate (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0004">4c</a>). The high pseudocapacitance behavior supports the remarkable rate capability of Sn-HNTO@C. From this perspective, the introduction of Sn-doping and OV enhances the pseudocapacitive behavior of the Sn-HNTO@C electrode, leading to superior rate performance.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202417725-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9f3fdb82-e541-4f25-802f-bf40697a9c81/adfm202417725-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/9f3fdb82-e541-4f25-802f-bf40697a9c81/adfm202417725-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/51c84a3d-01a8-4da7-9901-30f8ee453783/adfm202417725-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9f3fdb82-e541-4f25-802f-bf40697a9c81/adfm202417725-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202417725-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202417725" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves at various sweep rates for Sn-HNTO@C electrode. b) The <i>b</i> value of different oxidation and reduction peaks corresponding to the CV curves. c) The capacitance contribution ratio at different sweep rate for Sn-HNTO@C, Sn-NTO@C, HNTO@C, HNTO, and NTO electrodes. d) GITT profiles. The corresponding Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients during the e) discharge and f) charge processes. g) In situ EIS plots, (g<sub>1</sub>) DRT curves, and (g<sub>2</sub>) DRT intensity distribution graph of the Sn-HNTO@C during the first discharge/charge process. h) In situ EIS plots, h<sub>1</sub>) DRT curves, and h<sub>2</sub>) DRT intensity distribution graph of the Sn-HNTO@C during the third discharge/charge process. i) Tafel plots of HNTO@C, Sn-NTO@C, and Sn-HNTO@C electrodes. j) Arrhenius plot of <i>R<sub>ct</sub></i> contribution with the derived <i>E<sub>a</sub></i>. k) Illustration comparison of charge transfer energy barriers for HNTO@C, Sn-NTO@C, and Sn-HNTO@C electrodes.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The Na-ion diffusion kinetics of all electrodes were further investigated by galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) and EIS tests (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a>, Supporting Information). Obviously, the Sn-HNTO@C electrode displays a higher <i>D<sub>Na+</sub></i> during the charge/discharge phases compared to the Sn-NTO@C, HNTO@C, HNTO, and NTO electrodes, suggesting faster transport kinetics (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0004">4d−f</a>). Fitting results of the EIS curves at room temperature demonstrate that the Sn-HNTO@C electrode exhibits the lowest <i>R<sub>ct</sub></i> and <i>σ</i> value, suggesting the fastest diffusion rate of Na<sup>+</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28a,b</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, the evolution of the interfacial resistance of all electrodes was further revealed by in situ EIS combined with the distribution of relaxation time (DRT) analysis. In Nyquist plots (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0004">4g,h</a>), the <i>R<sub>ct</sub></i> represented by the semicircle decreases continuously from the OCV to full saturation (0.01V) as the discharge process proceeds. During the charging process, <i>R<sub>ct</sub></i> shows an increasing trend, indicating a stable intercalation reaction as well as favorable kinetics. As a model-free analysis of impedance changes, the DRT technique allows the analysis of specific electrochemical processes on different time scales.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0040_R_d43537240e1298" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J. Power Sources">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Four dominant peaks appear in the DRT curves during the first cycle and third cycle (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0004">4g1,h1</a>), which are labeled as P1, P2, P3, and P4. The time constant of the P1 peaks is ≈10<sup>−5</sup> s, corresponding to the internal resistance including the resistances of the electrolyte solution and electrodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0041_R_d43537240e1311" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - J. Power Sources">41</a>, <a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0042_R_d43537240e1311" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Energy Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The P2 peaks distributed 10<sup>−4</sup> s, representing the impedance of SEI.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0043_R_d43537240e1319" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Chem">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the first cycle, P2 peaks represent an irreversible evolution, which can be attributed to the SEI formation (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0044_R_d43537240e1327" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Joule">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the third cycle, the P2 peaks show a high degree of overlap, indicating the robustness of the SEI layer (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0004">4g2,h2</a>). Based on timescale analyses, the remaining P3 (≈10<sup>−3</sup> s) and P4 (≈10<sup>−2</sup> s) peaks correspond to the <i>R<sub>ct</sub></i> and diffusion impedance, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0045_R_d43537240e1347" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Energy">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In comparison to other electrodes (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30−S33</a>, Supporting Information), Sn-HNTO@C exhibits minimal impedance both before and after cycling, indicating efficient Na<sup>+</sup> storage kinetics and excellent electrochemical performance.</p>
            
            <p>Tafel curves of Sn-HNTO@C, Sn-NTO@C, HNTO@C, HNTO, and NTO are gathered, with corresponding exchange current densities of 1.68, 1.14, 0.95, 0.76, and 0.59 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0004">4i</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S34</a>, Supporting Information). This also proves that the Sn-doping and OV improve the electron transfer kinetics and support its excellent rate capability. Beneficial from the low charge transfer resistance at different temperatures, the Sn-HNTO@C electrode exhibits lower activation energy (<i>E<sub>a</sub></i>) during the interfacial charge transfer process (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S35</a>, Supporting Information; Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0004">4j,k</a>), which corresponds to the above kinetic results. The morphology and structure evolution of all electrodes during cycling were detected by ex situ SEM. Compared to the other electrodes, the Sn-HNTO@C electrode after 200 cycles also maintains an intact spherical structure (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S36</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S37</a>, Supporting Information). TEM test results further show that ultra-thin Sn-HNTO nanosheets are dispersed within the hollow carbon spheres (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S38</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that high conductivity of Sn-HNTO@C electrode is favorable to sustain the volume change during repeated Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction to guarantee structural integrity.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202417725-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202417725-sec-0070-title">2.5 Low Temperature Electrolyte Optimization and Sodium Storage Performance</h3>
            
            <p>Benefiting from the synergistic effect of Sn doping and OV, the Sn-HNTO@C electrode exhibits enhanced sodium storage performance and rapid transport kinetics in ester-based electrolytes at room temperature. However, at low temperatures, the increase in viscosity of ester-based electrolytes leads to a decrease in ion conductivity, an increase in interfacial charge transfer impedance, and slow kinetic transport of Na<sup>+</sup> within the SEI film, ultimately reducing the energy density and cycling performance of the battery and limiting its application under low temperature conditions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0046_R_d43537240e1403" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Energy Environ. Sci.">46</a>, <a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0047_R_d43537240e1403" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Adv. Mater.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, designing appropriate electrolytes is the key to improving the low-temperature electrochemical performance of SIBs. Compared with ester-based electrolytes, ether-based electrolytes have better reduction stability and decomposition resistance at low temperatures, which can improve the kinetics of low-temperature interfacial charge transfer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0048_R_d43537240e1408" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Here, we choose two additional ether-based electrolytes and ester-based electrolytes as electrolyte electrodes to study their low-temperature electrochemical performance. First, the highest occupied molecular orbital (HOMO) and the lowest unoccupied molecular orbital (LUMO) energy levels of salts and solvents in different electrolytes were calculated using the frontier molecular orbital theory (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S39</a>, Supporting Information). The LUMO energy level of diglyme is not only higher than that of ester solvents but also the difference in the energy level of electron transfer from HOMO to LUMO is greater, suggesting its better reduction stability and resistance to decomposition (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0005">5a</a>). Therefore, sodium salts in diglyme-based electrolytes are more inclined to decompose and participate in the formation of SEI containing fluoride. Furthermore, the higher HOMO energy of diglyme solvent and the [NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>-Diglyme]<sup>+</sup> complex makes it easier to provide electrons and promote the formation of cathode electrolyte interface (CEI) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0005">5b</a>), improving the electrochemical performance of the full cell.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0049_R_d43537240e1433" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0005">5c</a> shows the electrostatic potential maps and weak interaction distributions of solvated Na<sup>+</sup> in different electrolyte systems, respectively. The blue portion indicates a negative potential caused by charge accumulation and the red portion indicates a positive potential. In the [NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>-Diglyme]<sup>+</sup> system, the charge distribution around Na<sup>+</sup> is significantly lighter, representing better solvation binding.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0050_R_d43537240e1452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Adv. Energy Mater.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The green part indicates the weak interaction distribution, where the bluish part indicates attraction and the reddish part indicates site resistance. In the [NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>-Diglyme]<sup>+</sup> system, the presence of a more robust weak interaction around Na<sup>+</sup> serves to reinforce the stability of the system. Calculations show that diglyme-based electrolyte displays a lower desolvation energy barrier compared to ester-based (EC/PC-based) electrolytes, which facilitates the desolvation behavior of interfacial Na<sup>+</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0005">5d</a>). Moreover, the Na<sup>+</sup> solvation configurations of different electrolytes at −15&nbsp;°C were investigated through ab initio molecular dynamics (AIMD) simulations (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0005">5e–g</a>). In the radial distribution function plot (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0005">5h</a>), Na-F[NaPF<sub>6</sub>-Diglyme] shows more prominent <i>g(r)</i>, suggesting stronger Na–F interactions between PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> in diglyme-based electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0051_R_d43537240e1490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Adv. Energy Mater.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Whereas Na–F[NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>-Diglyme]<sup>+</sup> preferred to show stronger interaction in the second solvation shell. This promotes the decomposition of CF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> rather than diglyme solvent, facilitating the formation of a fluorine-rich SEI layer at the electrode interface. Simultaneously, Na–O[NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>-Diglyme]<sup>+</sup> demonstrates a significant peak ≈2.4 Å and a weak peak ≈4.7 Å, indicating that diglyme is overwhelmingly distributed in the first solvation shell, which is more evident in Na–O[NaPF<sub>6</sub>-Diglyme].<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0052_R_d43537240e1516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Adv. Sci.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, Na–O[NaClO<sub>4</sub>-EC/PC] showed two distinct peaks, demonstrating that a greater number of carbonate molecules were coordinated with Na<sup>+</sup> to form a stable double-shell solvation structure. Compared to EC/PC-based electrolytes, the Na<sup>+</sup> solvation shell in diglyme-based electrolytes is smaller, leading to weaker repulsion between adjacent solvent molecules and accelerated interfacial reaction kinetics.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202417725-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/97757108-7268-428a-a182-3f604c1095ac/adfm202417725-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/97757108-7268-428a-a182-3f604c1095ac/adfm202417725-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0244073e-6777-4d8a-b306-8eb53989d000/adfm202417725-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/97757108-7268-428a-a182-3f604c1095ac/adfm202417725-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202417725-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202417725" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The LUMO/HOMO energy level diagram of different salts and solvents. b) LUMO and HOMO energy levels of salt-solvent complexes in various electrolytes. c) Electrostatic density and weak interaction distribution. d) Desolvation energy of Diglyme and EC/PC solvents. e−g) AIMD simulation snapshots of different electrolytes. h) Radial distribution functions <i>g(r)</i> of Na–F and Na–O pairs in three electrolytes. i,j) Comparison of cycling and rate capability of Sn-HNTO@C in different electrolytes at low temperatures. k,l) Cycling performance of Sn-HNTO@C at 0.3 A g<sup>−1</sup> under −15&nbsp;°C and corresponding discharge-charge curves. m) Comparison of low-temperature capacity with reported anodes of SIBs. n) Cycle performance of Sn-HNTO@C//NVPF full cell at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> under −15&nbsp;°C (inset: rate performance and photograph of powered LEDs).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>On this basis, these three electrolytes were further used to test the low-temperature performance of the Sn-HNTO@C electrode. Compared with ester-based electrolytes, Sn-HNTO@C exhibits better electrochemical performance in the diglyme-based electrolyte at −15&nbsp;°C. Especially, NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>/diglyme demonstrates the best cycle and rate performance, with a capacity of 214 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and a capacity of 72 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even at 3 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0005">5i,j</a>). At a higher current density of 0.3 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the Sn-HNTO@C electrode demonstrates sustained cycling stability over 500 cycles (177 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0005">5k,l</a>). Compared to previously reported anode materials for SIBs, Sn-HNTO@C shows the highest low-temperature capacity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0005">5m</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, Sn-HNTO@C exhibits low polarization in the NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>/diglyme electrolyte, proving that it possessed better ionic/electronic dynamics (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S40</a>, Supporting Information). GITT test shows that Sn-HNTO@C has higher <i>D<sub>Na+</sub></i> in NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>/diglyme electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S41</a>, Supporting Information). EIS test results show that Sn-HNTO@C exhibits smaller <i>R<sub>ct</sub></i> in ether-based electrolytes compared to ester-based electrolytes, which implies that it has a faster charge transfer rate (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S42a,b</a>, Supporting Information). Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S42c</a> (Supporting Information) illustrates the linear correlation between <i>ω<sup>−1/2</sup></i> and <i>Z′</i>, as determined by the fitting process.
