According to Equations <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information), the calculated <i>σ</i> value is the lowest, suggesting the fastest diffusion rate of Na<sup>+</sup>. This is in agreement with the GITT results. This also indicates that ether-based electrolytes can achieve high ionic conductivity, and improve interfacial charge transfer kinetics at low temperatures. Therefore, NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>/diglyme was chosen as the electrolyte for low-temperature SIB full cells. The assembled Sn-HNTO@C//NVPF full cell demonstrates satisfactory electrochemical performance when tested at −15&nbsp;°C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S43</a>, Supporting Information). It maintains a discharge capacity of 91 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and a capacity of 71 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The practical applicability was demonstrated by utilizing the assembled full cell to power LEDs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0005">5n</a>). The excellent electrochemical properties of Sn-HNTO@C at low temperatures indicate that it is a suitable anode material for a wide range of environments.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202417725-sec-0080">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202417725-sec-0080-title">2.6 Low Temperature Sodium Storage Mechanism and Interfacial Chemistry</h3>
            
            <p>The interface Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation step is the main step determining the rate capability at low temperatures because of the considerably high desolvation energy barrier.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0053_R_d43537240e1663" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Reported studies have demonstrated that graphite anodes display ion-solvent co-intercalation chemical behavior when operating in ether-based electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0054_R_d43537240e1668" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared with graphite anodes, Sn-HNTO@C exhibits a stable structural framework and larger interlayer spacing, which is expected to avoid the desolvation process, maintain structural stability during Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent insertion/extraction, and achieve rapid kinetics at low temperatures. To deeply investigate the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism at low temperatures, the Sn-HNTO@C electrode was tested using ex situ XRD and Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR). Obviously, (200) peak in ex situ XRD test suffered a significant change in position during charge/discharge, suggesting that the intercalation and deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> leads to the lattice deformation of Sn-HNTO@C (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0006">6a,b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S44</a>, Supporting Information). Due to the superior low temperature rate performance of Sn-HNTO@C, we doubt that the ion-solvent co-intercalation may have resulted in further expansion and contraction of the Sn-HNTO@C crystal structure. To verify this hypothesis, the FTIR spectra of NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> in diglyme electrolyte and the initial electrode were compared, and a significant peak at 858 cm<sup>−1</sup> was found, which should belong to the solvated Na<sup>+</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0006">6c</a>). The distinctive peaks associated with the solvated Na<sup>+</sup> are observed during the initial complete discharge process and subsequently disappear upon recharging to 2.5 V for Sn-HNTO@C. If the solvent is only adsorbed on the electrode surface, the signal remains constant regardless of the charging/discharging state and can be collected throughout the whole charge/discharge process. The aforementioned evidence suggests that a Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent co-intercalation effect is present during the charge transfer process at low temperatures, which can avoid the high desolvation energy barrier and remarkedly enhance the sodium storage capacity of Sn-HNTO@C at low temperatures (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0006">6d</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202417725-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e85e8a6a-8b0a-4f17-b67c-3089a2c9c236/adfm202417725-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e85e8a6a-8b0a-4f17-b67c-3089a2c9c236/adfm202417725-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4be9674d-4c56-40a7-a713-a9ea7773e23a/adfm202417725-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e85e8a6a-8b0a-4f17-b67c-3089a2c9c236/adfm202417725-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202417725-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202417725" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Characterization of Na-storage mechanisms and component analysis of SEI formed at low temperatures. a) Ex situ XRD patterns, and b) corresponding partial enlargement of Sn-HNTO@C electrode operating at −15&nbsp;°C. c) Ex situ FTIR spectra for Sn-HNTO@C. d) Schematic of Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent co-intercalation mechanism for Sn-HNTO@C at low temperatures. e−g) High-resolution C 1s, F 1s, and O 1s XPS spectra of Sn-HNTO@C electrode after Ar ion sputtering with different time. h−j) The corresponding 3D high-resolution XPS spectra maps. k) The atomic ratio evolution of C, F, O and Na elements with time profiling in Sn-HNTO@C electrode. l) TEM images of Sn-HNTO@C electrode cycled at −15&nbsp;°C. m) TOF-SIMS sputter depth profile of various secondary ion species. n,o) Corresponding chemical mapping and 3D view images. p) Schematic illustration of the SEI chemistry at low temperatures.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The chemical components of the SEI layer on Sn-HNTO@C after cycling at −15&nbsp;°C were studied by Ar<sup>+</sup>-etched XPS analysis. The high-resolution C 1s and F 1s spectra of the Sn-HNTO@C electrode are depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0006">6e,f</a>. The characteristic peaks of C–C (284.8 eV), RCH<sub>2</sub>–F (288.5 eV), and –CF<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup> (291.8 eV in C 1s, 688.2 eV in F 1s) decrease significantly after the first etching, indicating that fluorinated organic components dominate the SEI layer surface. Whereas the enhancement of the C–O (286.1 eV) peak as well as the recovery of the C–C peak with the increase of etching time indicate the growth of organic compounds. The C 1s spectra are also fitted using peaks with binding energies of 282.6 (C–Ti), 287.8 (C═O), and 289.5 (O–C═O) eV. As etching proceeds, the peak of –CF<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup> diminishes to disappear, while the peak of Na–F (684.0 eV) becomes visible. The existence of NaF not only contributes to the stability of the SEI layer but also provides a fast channel for the transfer of Na<sup>+</sup>, thus improving the electrochemical performance of Sn-HNTO@C anode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0055_R_d43537240e1750" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Nat. Energy">55</a>, <a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0056_R_d43537240e1750" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to the O 1s XPS spectrum, the peak of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> is progressively fading, indicating that this compound mainly exists on the surface of the SEI layer, which is in agreement with the fitting results of the C 1s spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0006">6g</a>). In addition, Na–O–Ti (527.8 eV), Na–O (529.8 eV), and R–O–Na (533.2 eV) peaks were fitted in the O1s spectra. The above analysis indicates that the diglyme-derived SEI film at low temperatures mainly contains high elasticity and stable organic species, leading to superior low temperature performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0057_R_d43537240e1763" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peak situated at 1070.1 eV in Na 1s spectra signifies the Na–F and Na─O bond, demonstrating the existence of sodium compounds (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S45a</a>, Supporting Information). To reveal more clearly the evolution of chemical states on the electrode surface, 3D high-resolution C 1s, F 1s, O1s, and Na 1s XPS spectra maps of Sn-HNTO@C electrode at different etching times are shown in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0006">6h–j</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S45b</a> (Supporting Information). The atomic ratios of C, F, O, and Na elements with etching depth are summarized in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0006">6k</a>. The tendency of the F element to decrease first and then increase during the etching process confirms that fluorine-containing organics are weakened at the initial stage and NaF is continuously produced. Meanwhile, after five cycles, the structure of Sn-HNTO@C remains intact and the distribution of F is proven by EDS elemental mappings (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S46</a>, Supporting Information). The TEM results indicate that an organic–inorganic SEI layer with a thickness of ≈10 nm has been successfully formed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0006">6l</a>). The indistinct lattice fringes should be attributed to inorganic substances such as Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, NaF, and Na<sub>2</sub>O, which is supported by the XPS results. The fast Fourier transform (FFT) results indicate that these inorganic compounds are uniformly distributed in an amorphous form, which enhances the ionic conductivity of the material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0058_R_d43537240e1794" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Energy Environ. Sci.">58</a>, <a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0059_R_d43537240e1794" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Chem. Mat.">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Obviously, this thinner, organic-rich and amorphous inorganic SEI film is beneficial for the rapid diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> at low temperatures.</p>
            
            <p>In addition, the detailed ingredients of the SEI layer formed on Sn-HNTO@C at −15&nbsp;°C, were further performed by TOF-SIMS. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S47</a> (Supporting Information) displays the negative ion mode, which further deciphered the fragments of the SEI layer and provided additional chemical composition information. As illustrated in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0006">6m</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S48</a> (Supporting Information), both inorganic and organic species were detected on the surfaces of the Sn-HNTO@C electrode. The S<sup>−</sup> and F<sup>−</sup> containing secondary ion fragments (e.g., SO<sup>2−</sup>, SO<sup>3−</sup>, and NaF<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup>) were attributed to the decomposition products of CF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>, whereas C-containing secondary ion fragments may be derived mainly from polycrystalline organic sodium salts composed of diglyme or its analogs and NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202417725-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202417725-bib-0060_R_d43537240e1835" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Nano Energy">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> With the sputter time increases, the intensity of NaF<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup> increases and then decreases, SO<sup>2−</sup>, SO<sup>3−</sup>, and CF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> decreases, while signals of the vast majority of organic fragments remained constant regardless or increases accordingly, indicating the existence of an alternating organic–inorganic boundary layer in the formed SEI. The distribution for components on the cycled Sn-HNTO@C surface at −15&nbsp;°C is more apparent in the chemical mapping and corresponding 3D view images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0006">6n,o</a>). Obviously, compared to inorganic species, organic species have a richer variety and a more uniform distribution. To visually observe the SEI structure on Sn-HNTO@C, a schematic diagram of its composition and distribution was constructed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202417725-fig-0006">6p</a>). Overall, the SEI films mainly composed of organic components and containing certain amorphous inorganic substances are beneficial for enhancing the interfacial reaction kinetics and realizing the Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent co-intercalation storage mechanism.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202417725-sec-0090">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202417725-sec-0090-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we have meticulously designed and manufactured heteroelement Sn-doping and oxygen vacancy rich Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> ultrathin nanosheets confined in porous hollow carbon spheres (Sn-HNTO@C) composite to enhance low-temperature sodium storage performance. Theoretical calculations and electrochemical kinetics studies demonstrate that heteroelement appropriate Sn-doping and vacancy defects can improve conductivity, enhance Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption, and accelerate interface reaction kinetics. Optimizing the electrolyte revealed the formation of homogeneous and robust SEI in ether-based electrolytes at low temperatures, containing more organic components and inorganic particles, thereby achieving rapid Na<sup>+</sup> interface kinetics at low temperatures. The special Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent co-intercalation mechanism with lower charge transfer activation energy, and lower Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barriers endows the Sn-HNTO@C anode with superior low temperature sodium storage performance. More importantly, the Sn-HNTO@C//NVPF full cell also delivers high capacity and cycle stability under −15&nbsp;°C. This work provides valuable insights for the construction of advanced titanium-based anode materials in a wide temperature range for SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false" class="visitable" target="_blank">Web of Science®</a></span><span><a class="google-scholar visitable"
